<p><b>Ivory Coast</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/34/En-us-Ivory_Coast.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span>) or <b>Côte d'Ivoire</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˌ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'k' in 'kind'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">k</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/oʊ/ long 'o' in 'bode'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">oʊ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'t' in 'tie'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">t</span></a></span> <span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for dotted">ɨ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'v' in 'vie'" style="border-bottom:1px href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span class="SMALLCAPS" style="FONT-VARIANT:SMALL-CAPS;"><span class="NOCAPS" style="TEXT-TRANSFORM:LOWERCASE;">KOHT</span></span></span></b></i></a></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling" class="Unicode"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Wikipedia:Pronunciation respelling key"><i>dee-<b><span class="smallcaps"><span class="SMALLCAPS" style="FONT-VARIANT:SMALL-CAPS;"><span class="NOCAPS" style="TEXT-TRANSFORM:LOWERCASE;">VWAHR</span></span></span></b></i></a></span>; <small>French:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[kot divwaʁ]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<a href="/wiki/File:Fr-C%C3%B4te-d%27Ivoire-fr-Paris.ogg" title="File:Fr-Côte-d'Ivoire-fr-Paris.ogg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" width="13" height="13" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a> <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/Fr-C%C3%B4te-d%27Ivoire-fr-Paris.ogg" class="internal" title="Fr-Côte-d'Ivoire-fr-Paris.ogg">listen</a>)</small>), officially the <b>Republic of Côte d'Ivoire</b> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr"><i>République de Côte d'Ivoire</i></span>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>. Ivory Coast's <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> capital is <a href="/wiki/Yamoussoukro" title="Yamoussoukro">Yamoussoukro</a> and the biggest city is the port city of <a href="/wiki/Abidjan" title="Abidjan">Abidjan</a>.</p>
<p>Prior to its colonization by Europeans, Ivory Coast was home to several states, including <a href="/wiki/Gyaaman" title="Gyaaman">Gyaaman</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kong_Empire" title="Kong Empire">Kong Empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baoul%C3%A9_people" title="Baoulé people">Baoulé</a>. There were two <a href="/wiki/Anyi_people" title="Anyi people">Anyi</a> kingdoms, <i>Indénié</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sanwi" title="Kingdom of Sanwi">Sanwi</a></i>, which attempted to retain their separate identity through the French colonial period and after independence.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Ivory Coast became a protectorate of France in 1843–44 and was later formed into a French colony in 1893 amid the European <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">scramble for Africa</a>. Ivory Coast achieved independence in 1960, led by <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Houphou%C3%ABt-Boigny" title="Félix Houphouët-Boigny">Félix Houphouët-Boigny</a>, who ruled the country until 1993. It maintained close political and economic association with its West African neighbours, while at the same time maintaining close ties to the West, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7afrique" title="Françafrique">especially France</a>. Since the end of Houphouët-Boigny's rule in 1993, Ivory Coast has experienced one <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a>, in 1999, and two religiously-grounded civil wars: the first taking place <a href="/wiki/First_Ivorian_Civil_War" title="First Ivorian Civil War">between 2002 and 2007</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> and the second <a href="/wiki/Second_Ivorian_Civil_War" title="Second Ivorian Civil War">during 2010-2011</a>.</p>
<p>Ivory Coast is a republic with a strong executive power invested in its <a href="/wiki/President_of_Ivory_Coast" title="President of Ivory Coast" class="mw-redirect">president</a>. Through the production of coffee and <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa</a>, the country was an economic powerhouse in West Africa during the 1960s and 1970s. Ivory Coast went through an economic crisis in the 1980s, contributing to a period of political and social turmoil. The 21st-century Ivorian economy is largely market-based and still relies heavily on agriculture, with <a href="/wiki/Smallholding" title="Smallholding">smallholder</a> cash-crop production being dominant.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> is French, with indigenous local languages also widely used, including <a href="/wiki/Baoul%C3%A9_language" title="Baoulé language">Baoulé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dioula_language" title="Dioula language" class="mw-redirect">Dioula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dan_language" title="Dan language">Dan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anyin_language" title="Anyin language">Anyin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Senari_languages" title="Senari languages">Cebaara Senufo</a>. The main religions are Islam, Christianity (primarily <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Ivory_Coast" title="Roman Catholicism in Ivory Coast">Roman Catholic</a>) and various <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_religion" title="Indigenous religion" class="mw-redirect">indigenous religions</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Portuguese and French merchant-explorers in the 15th and 16th centuries divided the west coast of Africa, very roughly, into five <i>coasts</i> reflecting local economies. The coast that the French named the <i>Côte d'Ivoire</i> and the Portuguese named the <i>Costa do Marfim</i>—both, literally, being "Ivory Coast"—lay between what was known as the <i>Guiné de Cabo Verde</i>, so-called "Upper Guinea" at <a href="/wiki/Cap-Vert" title="Cap-Vert">Cabo Verde</a>, and Lower Guinea.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThornton199653.E2.80.9356_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThornton199653.E2.80.9356-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipski200539_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipski200539-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> There were also a "<a href="/wiki/Grain_Coast" title="Grain Coast" class="mw-redirect">Grain Coast</a>", a "<a href="/wiki/Gold_Coast_(region)" title="Gold Coast (region)">Gold Coast</a>", and a "<a href="/wiki/Slave_Coast" title="Slave Coast">Slave Coast</a>", and, like those three, the name "Ivory Coast" reflected the major trade that occurred on that particular stretch of the coast: the export of ivory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuckett1853594_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuckett1853594-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThornton199653.E2.80.9356_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThornton199653.E2.80.9356-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHomans185814_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHomans185814-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipsky200539_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipsky200539-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPl.C3.A9e1868146_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPl.C3.A9e1868146-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Other names for the coast of ivory included the <i>Côte de Dents</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-teeths_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-teeths-17"><span>[</span>n 1<span>]</span></a></sup> literally "Teeth Coast", again reflecting the trade in ivory;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlanchard181857_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlanchard181857-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuckett1853594_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuckett1853594-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipski200539_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipski200539-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPl.C3.A9e1868146_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPl.C3.A9e1868146-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalckenaer182735_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalckenaer182735-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> the <i>Côte de Quaqua</i>, after the people that the Dutch named the Quaqua (alternatively Kwa Kwa);<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThornton199653.E2.80.9356_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThornton199653.E2.80.9356-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaiss.C3.A8te1755185.E2.80.93186_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaiss.C3.A8te1755185.E2.80.93186-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> the Coast of the Five and Six Stripes, after a type of cotton fabric also traded there;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <i>Côte du Vent</i><sup id="cite_ref-vent_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vent-21"><span>[</span>n 2<span>]</span></a></sup>, the Windward Coast, after perennial local off-shore weather conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuckett1853594_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuckett1853594-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThornton199653.E2.80.9356_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThornton199653.E2.80.9356-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> One can find the name <i>Cote de(s) Dents</i> regularly used in older works.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> It was used in Duckett's <i>Dictionnaire</i> (<a href="#CITEREFDuckett1853">Duckett 1853</a>) and by Nicolas Villault de Bellefond, for examples, although <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Fran%C3%A7ois_Pr%C3%A9vost" title="Antoine François Prévost">Antoine François Prévost</a> used <i>Côte d'Ivoire</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalckenaer182735_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalckenaer182735-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 19th century it died out in favour of <i>Côte d'Ivoire</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911100-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The coastline of the modern state is not quite coterminous with what the 15th- and 16th-century merchants knew as the "Teeth" or "Ivory" coast, which was considered to stretch from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Palmas" title="Cape Palmas">Cape Palmas</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Three_Points" title="Cape Three Points">Cape Three Points</a> and which is thus now divided between the modern states of Ghana and Ivory Coast (with a minute portion of Liberia).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlanchard181857_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlanchard181857-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHomans185814_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHomans185814-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalckenaer182735_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalckenaer182735-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaiss.C3.A8te1755185.E2.80.93186_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaiss.C3.A8te1755185.E2.80.93186-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> But it retained the name through French rule and independence in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-WDL1_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDL1-22"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> The name had long since been translated literally into other languages,<sup id="cite_ref-trans_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trans-24"><span>[</span>n 3<span>]</span></a></sup> which the post-independence government considered to be increasingly troublesome whenever its international dealings extended beyond the Francophone sphere. Therefore, in April 1986, the government declared Côte d'Ivoire (or, more fully, République de Côte d'Ivoire<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAuziasLabourdette20089_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAuziasLabourdette20089-25"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup>) to be its formal name for the purposes of diplomatic protocol, and officially refuses to recognize or accept any translation from French to another language in its international dealings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid20007_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid20007-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeaRowe2001127_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeaRowe2001127-26"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJessup1998351_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJessup1998351-27"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the Ivorian government's request, the English translation "Ivory Coast" (sometimes "the Ivory Coast") is still frequently used in English, by various media outlets and publications.<sup id="cite_ref-outlets_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-outlets-30"><span>[</span>n 4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-organizations_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-organizations-34"><span>[</span>n 5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ivory_Coast" title="History of Ivory Coast">History of Ivory Coast</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_migration">Land migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Land migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cistones.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Cistones.jpg/220px-Cistones.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Cistones.jpg/330px-Cistones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Cistones.jpg/440px-Cistones.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="673" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cistones.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Prehistoric polished stone <a href="/wiki/Celt_(tool)" title="Celt (tool)">celt</a> from <a href="/wiki/Boundiali" title="Boundiali">Boundiali</a> in northern Ivory Coast. Photo taken at the <a href="/wiki/IFAN_Museum_of_African_Arts" title="IFAN Museum of African Arts">IFAN Museum of African Arts</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">Dakar</a>, Senegal.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first human presence in Ivory Coast has been difficult to determine because human remains have not been well preserved in the country's humid climate. However, the presence of newly found weapon and tool fragments (specifically, polished axes cut through <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a> and remnants of cooking and fishing) has been interpreted as a possible indication of a large human presence during the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> period (15,000 to 10,000 BC),<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> or at the minimum, the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest known inhabitants of Ivory Coast have left traces scattered throughout the territory. Historians believe that they were all either displaced or absorbed by the ancestors of the present indigenous inhabitants, who migrated south into the area before the 16th century. Such groups included the Ehotilé (<a href="/wiki/Aboisso" title="Aboisso">Aboisso</a>), Kotrowou (Fresco), Zéhiri (<a href="/wiki/Grand_Lahou" title="Grand Lahou">Grand Lahou</a>), Ega and Diès (<a href="/wiki/Divo,_Ivory_Coast" title="Divo, Ivory Coast">Divo</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islamic_and_Islamic_periods">Pre-Islamic and Islamic periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pre-Islamic and Islamic periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first recorded history is found in the chronicles of North African (<a href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people" class="mw-redirect">Berber</a>) traders, who, from early <a href="/wiki/Roman_times" title="Roman times" class="mw-redirect">Roman times</a>, conducted a <a href="/wiki/Caravan_(travellers)" title="Caravan (travellers)">caravan</a> trade across the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> in salt, <a href="/wiki/Slaves" title="Slaves" class="mw-redirect">slaves</a>, gold, and other goods. The southern terminals of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">trans-Saharan trade</a> routes were located on the edge of the desert, and from there supplemental trade extended as far south as the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Rain_forest" title="Rain forest" class="mw-redirect">rain forest</a>. The more important terminals—<a href="/wiki/Djenn%C3%A9" title="Djenné">Djenné</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gao" title="Gao">Gao</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Timbuctu" title="Timbuctu" class="mw-redirect">Timbuctu</a>—grew into major commercial centres around which the great Sudanic empires developed.</p>
<p>By controlling the trade routes with their powerful military forces, these empires were able to dominate neighbouring states. The Sudanic empires also became centres of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_studies" title="Islamic studies">Islamic education</a>. Islam had been introduced in the western Sudan (today's Mali) by Muslim Berber traders from North Africa; it spread rapidly after the conversion of many important rulers. From the 11th century, by which time the rulers of the Sudanic empires had embraced Islam, it spread south into the northern areas of contemporary Ivory Coast.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ghana_empire" title="Ghana empire" class="mw-redirect">Ghana empire</a>, the earliest of the Sudanic empires, flourished in present-day eastern Mauritania from the fourth to the 13th century. At the peak of its power in the 11th century, its realms extended from the Atlantic Ocean to Timbuctu. After the decline of Ghana, the <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a> grew into a powerful Muslim state, which reached its apogee in the early part of the 14th century. The territory of the Mali Empire in Ivory Coast was limited to the north-west corner around <a href="/wiki/Odienn%C3%A9" title="Odienné">Odienné</a>.</p>
<p>Its slow decline starting at the end of the 14th century followed internal discord and revolts by vassal states, one of which, <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai</a>, flourished as an empire between the fourteenth and sixteenth centuries. Songhai was also weakened by internal discord, which led to factional warfare. This discord spurred most of the migrations of peoples southward toward the forest belt. The dense rain forest, covering the southern half of the country, created barriers to the large-scale political organizations that had arisen in the north. Inhabitants lived in villages or clusters of villages; their contacts with the outside world were filtered through long-distance traders. Villagers subsisted on agriculture and hunting.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-European_era">Pre-European era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pre-European era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royaumes_ci.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Royaumes_ci.jpg/350px-Royaumes_ci.jpg" width="350" height="347" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Royaumes_ci.jpg/525px-Royaumes_ci.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Royaumes_ci.jpg/700px-Royaumes_ci.jpg 2x" data-file-width="810" data-file-height="802" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royaumes_ci.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pre-European kingdoms.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Five important states flourished in Ivory Coast in the pre-European era. The Muslim <a href="/wiki/Kong_Empire" title="Kong Empire">Kong Empire</a> was established by the <a href="/wiki/Juula" title="Juula" class="mw-redirect">Juula</a> in the early 18th century in the north-central region inhabited by the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9noufo" title="Sénoufo" class="mw-redirect">Sénoufo</a>, who had fled <a href="/wiki/Islamization" title="Islamization">Islamization</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a>. Although Kong became a prosperous center of agriculture, trade, and crafts, ethnic diversity and religious discord gradually weakened the kingdom. The city of Kong was destroyed in 1895 by <a href="/wiki/Samori_Ture" title="Samori Ture">Samori Ture</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Abron_tribe" title="Abron tribe">Abron</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Gyaaman" title="Gyaaman">Gyaaman</a> was established in the 17th century by an <a href="/wiki/Akan_people" title="Akan people">Akan</a> group, the Abron, who had fled the developing <a href="/wiki/The_Ashanti" title="The Ashanti" class="mw-redirect">Ashanti</a> <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Asanteman" title="Asanteman" class="mw-redirect">Asanteman</a> in what is present-day Ghana. From their settlement south of <a href="/wiki/Bondoukou" title="Bondoukou">Bondoukou</a>, the Abron gradually extended their hegemony over the <a href="/wiki/Dyula_people" title="Dyula people">Dyula people</a> in Bondoukou, who were recent émigrés from the market city of <a href="/wiki/Begho" title="Begho" class="mw-redirect">Begho</a>. Bondoukou developed into a major centre of commerce and Islam. The kingdom's <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quranic</a> scholars attracted students from all parts of West Africa. In the mid-17th century in east-central Ivory Coast, other Akan groups' fleeing the Asante established a <a href="/wiki/Baoul%C3%A9_people" title="Baoulé people">Baoulé</a> kingdom at <a href="/wiki/Sakasso" title="Sakasso" class="mw-redirect">Sakasso</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Anyi_people" title="Anyi people">Agni</a> kingdoms, Indénié and <a href="/wiki/Sanwi" title="Sanwi" class="mw-redirect">Sanwi</a>.</p>
<p>The Baoulé, like the Ashanti, developed a highly centralized political and administrative structure under three successive rulers. It finally split into smaller chiefdoms. Despite the breakup of their kingdom, the Baoulé strongly resisted French subjugation. The descendants of the rulers of the Agni kingdoms tried to retain their separate identity long after Ivory Coast's independence; as late as 1969, the Sanwi attempted to break away from Ivory Coast and form an independent kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Library_of_Congress_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Library_of_Congress-38"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> The current king of Sanwi is Nana Amon Ndoufou V (since 2002).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_French_rule">Establishment of French rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Establishment of French rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Compared to neighbouring Ghana, Ivory Coast suffered little from the <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade" class="mw-redirect">slave trade</a>, as European slaving and merchant ships preferred other areas along the coast with better harbours. The earliest recorded European voyage to West Africa was made by the Portuguese and took place in 1482. The first West African French settlement, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Louis,_Senegal" title="Saint Louis, Senegal" class="mw-redirect">Saint Louis</a>, was founded in the mid-17th century in Senegal while, at about the same time, the Dutch ceded to the French a settlement at <a href="/wiki/Gor%C3%A9e" title="Gorée">Goree Island</a>, off <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">Dakar</a>. A French <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">mission</a> was established in 1637 <a href="/wiki/Assinie" title="Assinie">Assinie</a> near the border with the <a href="/wiki/Gold_Coast_(British_colony)" title="Gold Coast (British colony)">Gold Coast</a> (now Ghana).</p>
<p>Assinie's survival was precarious, however it was not until the mid-19th century that the French were firmly established in Ivory Coast. In 1843–4, French admiral <a href="/wiki/Louis_Edouard_Bou%C3%ABt-Willaumez" title="Louis Edouard Bouët-Willaumez" class="mw-redirect">Bouët-Willaumez</a> signed treaties with the kings of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Bassam" title="Grand Bassam" class="mw-redirect">Grand Bassam</a> and Assinie regions, making their territories a French <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a>. French <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">explorers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missionaries" title="Missionaries" class="mw-redirect">missionaries</a>, trading companies, and soldiers gradually extended the area under French control inland from the lagoon region. Pacification was not accomplished until 1915.</p>
<p>Activity along the coast stimulated European interest in the interior, especially along the two great rivers, the <a href="/wiki/Senegal_River" title="Senegal River">Senegal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger</a>. Concerted French exploration of West Africa began in the mid-19th century but moved slowly, based more on individual initiative than on government policy. In the 1840s, the French concluded a series of treaties with local West African rulers that enabled the French to build fortified posts along the Gulf of Guinea to serve as permanent trading centres.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aouabou-Trait%C3%A9-1892.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Aouabou-Trait%C3%A9-1892.jpg/220px-Aouabou-Trait%C3%A9-1892.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Aouabou-Trait%C3%A9-1892.jpg/330px-Aouabou-Trait%C3%A9-1892.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Aouabou-Trait%C3%A9-1892.jpg/440px-Aouabou-Trait%C3%A9-1892.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="555" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aouabou-Trait%C3%A9-1892.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Louis-Gustave_Binger" title="Louis-Gustave Binger" class="mw-redirect">Louis-Gustave Binger</a> of French West Africa in 1892 treaty signing with <a href="/wiki/Famienkro" title="Famienkro">Famienkro</a> leaders, in present-day N'zi-Comoé Region, Ivory Coast.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first posts in Ivory Coast included one at Assinie and another at Grand Bassam, which became the colony's first capital. The treaties provided for French sovereignty within the posts, and for trading privileges in exchange for fees or <i><a href="/wiki/Coutume" title="Coutume">coutumes</a></i> paid annually to the local rulers for the use of the land. The arrangement was not entirely satisfactory to the French, because trade was limited and misunderstandings over treaty obligations often arose. Nevertheless, the French government maintained the treaties, hoping to expand trade.</p>
<p>France also wanted to maintain a presence in the region to stem the increasing influence of the British along the Gulf of Guinea coast. The French built naval bases to keep out non-French traders and began a systematic conquest of the interior. (They accomplished this only after a long war in the 1890s against <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Mandinka</a> forces, mostly from Gambia. <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">Guerrilla warfare</a> by the Baoulé and other eastern groups continued until 1917).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The defeat of France in the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a> in 1871 and the subsequent annexation by Germany of the French province of <a href="/wiki/Alsace_Lorraine" title="Alsace Lorraine" class="mw-redirect">Alsace Lorraine</a> caused the French government to abandon its colonial ambitions and withdraw its military garrisons from its French West African trading posts, leaving them in the care of resident merchants. The trading post at Grand Bassam in Ivory Coast was left in the care of a shipper from <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Verdier" title="Arthur Verdier">Arthur Verdier</a>, who in 1878 was named <a href="/wiki/Resident_(title)" title="Resident (title)">Resident</a> of the Establishment of Ivory Coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Library_of_Congress_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Library_of_Congress-38"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1886, to support its claims of effective occupation, France again assumed direct control of its West African coastal trading posts and embarked on an accelerated program of exploration in the interior. In 1887 Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Louis_Gustave_Binger" title="Louis Gustave Binger">Louis Gustave Binger</a> began a two-year journey that traversed parts of Ivory Coast's interior. By the end of the journey, he had concluded four treaties establishing French protectorates in Ivory Coast. Also in 1887, Verdier's agent, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Treich-Lapl%C3%A8ne" title="Marcel Treich-Laplène">Marcel Treich-Laplène</a>, negotiated five additional agreements that extended French influence from the headwaters of the Niger River Basin through Ivory Coast.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_colonial_era">French colonial era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: French colonial era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arriv%C3%A9e_%C3%A0_Kong-1892.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Arriv%C3%A9e_%C3%A0_Kong-1892.jpg/220px-Arriv%C3%A9e_%C3%A0_Kong-1892.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Arriv%C3%A9e_%C3%A0_Kong-1892.jpg/330px-Arriv%C3%A9e_%C3%A0_Kong-1892.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Arriv%C3%A9e_%C3%A0_Kong-1892.jpg/440px-Arriv%C3%A9e_%C3%A0_Kong-1892.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="533" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arriv%C3%A9e_%C3%A0_Kong-1892.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arrival in <a href="/wiki/Kong,_C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire" title="Kong, Côte d'Ivoire" class="mw-redirect">Kong</a> of new French West Africa governor Louis-Gustave Binger in 1892.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the end of the 1880s, France had established what passed for control over the coastal regions of Ivory Coast, and in 1889 Britain recognized French sovereignty in the area. That same year, France named Treich-Laplène titular governor of the territory. In 1893 Ivory Coast was made a French colony, and then Captain Binger was appointed governor. Agreements with Liberia in 1892 and with Britain in 1893 determined the eastern and western boundaries of the colony, but the northern boundary was not fixed until 1947 because of efforts by the French government to attach parts of Upper Volta (present-day <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a>) and <a href="/wiki/French_Sudan" title="French Sudan">French Sudan</a> (present-day Mali) to Ivory Coast for economic and administrative reasons.</p>
<p>France's main goal was to stimulate the production of exports. Coffee, cocoa and palm oil crops were soon planted along the coast. Ivory Coast stood out as the only West African country with a sizeable population of settlers; elsewhere in West and Central Africa, the French and British were largely bureaucrats.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As a result, French citizens owned one third of the cocoa, coffee and banana <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a> and adopted a forced-labour system.</p>
<p>Throughout the early years of French rule, French military contingents were sent inland to establish new posts. Some of the native population resisted French penetration and settlement. Among those offering greatest resistance was <a href="/wiki/Samori_Ture" title="Samori Ture">Samori Ture</a>, who in the 1880s and 1890s was establishing the <a href="/wiki/Wassoulou_Empire" title="Wassoulou Empire">Wassoulou Empire</a>, which extended over large parts of present-day Guinea, Mali, Burkina Faso, and Ivory Coast. Samori Ture's large, well-equipped army, which could manufacture and repair its own <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearms</a>, attracted strong support throughout the region. The French responded to Samori Ture's expansion of regional control with military pressure. French campaigns against Samori Ture, which were met with fierce resistance, intensified in the mid-1890s until he was captured in 1898.</p>
<p>France's imposition of a <a href="/wiki/Tax_per_head" title="Tax per head">head tax</a> in 1900 to support the colony in a <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">public works</a> program, provoked a number of revolts. Ivoirians viewed the tax as a violation of the terms of the protectorate treaties, because they thought that France was demanding the equivalent of a <i><a href="/wiki/Coutume" title="Coutume">coutume</a></i> from the local kings, rather than the reverse. Much of the population, especially in the interior, considered the tax a humiliating symbol of submission.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-39"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1905, the French officially abolished <a href="/wiki/African_slave_trade" title="African slave trade" class="mw-redirect">slavery</a> in most of French West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg/220px-Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg/330px-Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg 2x" data-file-width="346" data-file-height="457" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Samori Touré</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1904 to 1958, Ivory Coast was a constituent unit of the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_French_West_Africa" title="Federation of French West Africa" class="mw-redirect">Federation of French West Africa</a>. It was a colony and an overseas territory under the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">Third Republic</a>. In World War I Ivory Coast was put into a bad position with the German invasion threatened in 1914. But after France made regiments from Ivory Coast to fight in France. Coloney resources were rationed from 1917-1919. Some 150,000 men from Ivory Coast died in World War I. Until the period following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, governmental affairs in French West Africa were administered from Paris. France's policy in West Africa was reflected mainly in its philosophy of "association", meaning that all Africans in Ivory Coast were officially French "subjects", but without rights to representation in Africa or France.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empires#Second_French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empires" class="mw-redirect">French colonial policy</a> incorporated concepts of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilation</a> and association. Based on an assumption of the superiority of <a href="/wiki/French_culture" title="French culture" class="mw-redirect">French culture</a> over all others, in practice the assimilation policy meant extension of the French language, institutions, laws, and customs in the colonies. The policy of association also affirmed the superiority of the French in the colonies, but it entailed different institutions and systems of laws for the colonizer and the colonized. Under this policy, the Africans in Ivory Coast were allowed to preserve their own customs insofar as they were compatible with French interests.</p>
<p>An indigenous <a href="/wiki/Elite" title="Elite">elite</a> trained in French administrative practice formed an intermediary group between the French and the Africans. Assimilation was practiced in Ivory Coast to the extent that after 1930, a small number of Westernized Ivoirians were granted the right to apply for French citizenship. Most Ivoirians, however, were classified as French subjects and were governed under the principle of association.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-41"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> As subjects of France, they had no political rights. They were drafted for work in mines, on plantations, as porters, and on public projects as part of their tax responsibility. They were expected to serve in the military and were subject to the <i><a href="/wiki/Indig%C3%A9nat" title="Indigénat">indigénat</a></i>, a separate system of law.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-42"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In World War II, the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_regime" title="Vichy regime" class="mw-redirect">Vichy regime</a> remained in control until 1942,when British troops invaded without much resistance. Winston Churchill gave the power back to members of General <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>'s provisional government. By 1943 the Allies had returned French West Africa back to the French. The <a href="/wiki/Brazzaville_Conference_of_1944" title="Brazzaville Conference of 1944">Brazzaville Conference of 1944</a>, the first Constituent Assembly of the <a href="/wiki/French_Fourth_Republic" title="French Fourth Republic">Fourth Republic</a> in 1946, and France's gratitude for African loyalty during World War II led to far-reaching governmental reforms in 1946. French citizenship was granted to all African "subjects," the right to organize politically was recognized, and various forms of forced labour were abolished.</p>
<p>Until 1958, governors appointed in Paris administered the colony of Ivory Coast, using a system of direct, centralized administration that left little room for Ivoirian participation in policy making. Whereas British colonial administration adopted divide-and-rule policies elsewhere, applying ideas of assimilation only to the educated elite, the French were interested in ensuring that the small but influential elite was sufficiently satisfied with the status quo to refrain from any anti-French sentiment. Although strongly opposed to the practices of association, educated Ivoirians believed that they would achieve equality with their French peers through assimilation rather than through complete independence from France. But, after the assimilation doctrine was implemented entirely through the postwar reforms, Ivoirian leaders realized that even assimilation implied the superiority of the French over the Ivoirians, and that discrimination and political inequality would end only with independence.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-43"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Houphouet-Boigny_Kennedy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Houphouet-Boigny_Kennedy.jpg/220px-Houphouet-Boigny_Kennedy.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Houphouet-Boigny_Kennedy.jpg/330px-Houphouet-Boigny_Kennedy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Houphouet-Boigny_Kennedy.jpg/440px-Houphouet-Boigny_Kennedy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="760" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Houphouet-Boigny_Kennedy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Félix Houphouët-Boigny in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> <a href="/wiki/Entrance_Hall" title="Entrance Hall">Entrance Hall</a> with President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> in 1962</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The son of a Baoulé chief, <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Houphou%C3%ABt-Boigny" title="Félix Houphouët-Boigny">Félix Houphouët-Boigny</a>, was to become Ivory Coast's father of independence. In 1944 he formed the country's first agricultural trade union for African cocoa farmers like himself. Angered that colonial policy favoured French plantation owners, they united to recruit migrant workers for their own farms. Houphouët-Boigny soon rose to prominence and within a year was elected to the French Parliament in Paris. A year later the French abolished forced labour. Houphouët-Boigny established a strong relationship with the French government, expressing a belief that the country would benefit from it, which it did for many years. France appointed him as the first African to become a minister in a European government.</p>
<p>A turning point in relations with France was reached with the 1956 Overseas Reform Act (<i><a href="/wiki/Loi_Cadre" title="Loi Cadre" class="mw-redirect">Loi Cadre</a></i>), which transferred a number of powers from Paris to elected territorial governments in <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a> and also removed remaining voting inequalities. In 1958, Ivory Coast became an autonomous member of the French Community (which replaced the <a href="/wiki/French_Union" title="French Union">French Union</a>).</p>
<p>At the time of Ivory Coast's independence (1960), the country was easily French West Africa's most prosperous, contributing over 40% of the region's total exports. When Houphouët-Boigny became the first president, his government gave farmers good prices for their products to further stimulate production. This was further boosted by a significant immigration of workers from surrounding countries. Coffee production increased significantly, catapulting Ivory Coast into third place in world output (behind Brazil and Colombia). By 1979, the country was the world's leading producer of cocoa.</p>
<p>It also became Africa's leading exporter of <a href="/wiki/Pineapples" title="Pineapples" class="mw-redirect">pineapples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>. French technicians contributed to the 'Ivoirian miracle'. In other African nations, the people drove out the Europeans following independence; but in Ivory Coast, they poured in. The French community grew from only 30,000 prior to independence to 60,000 in 1980, most of them teachers, managers and advisors.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> For 20 years, the economy maintained an annual growth rate of nearly 10%—the highest of Africa's non-oil-exporting countries.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Houphou.C3.ABt-Boigny_administration">Houphouët-Boigny administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Houphouët-Boigny administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Houphouët-Boigny's one-party rule was not amenable to political competition. <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Gbagbo" title="Laurent Gbagbo">Laurent Gbagbo</a>, who would be the president of Ivory Coast in 2000, had to flee as he incurred the ire of Houphouët-Boigny when Gbagbo founded the <a href="/wiki/Ivoirian_Popular_Front" title="Ivoirian Popular Front" class="mw-redirect">Front Populaire Ivoirien</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Houphouët-Boigny banked on his broad appeal to the population who continually elected him. He was also criticized for his emphasis on developing large scale projects.</p>
<p>Many felt the millions of dollars spent transforming his home village, <a href="/wiki/Yamoussoukro" title="Yamoussoukro">Yamoussoukro</a>, into the new capital that it became, were wasted; others supported his vision to develop a centre for peace, education and religion in the heart of the country. But in the early 1980s, the world recession and a local drought sent shock waves through the Ivoirian economy. Due to the overcutting of timber and collapsing sugar prices, the country's external debt increased threefold. Crime rose dramatically in Abidjan.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1990, hundreds of civil servants went on strike, joined by students protesting institutional corruption. The unrest forced the government to support multi-party democracy. Houphouët-Boigny became increasingly feeble and died in 1993. He favoured <a href="/wiki/Henri_Konan_B%C3%A9di%C3%A9" title="Henri Konan Bédié">Henri Konan Bédié</a> as his successor.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="B.C3.A9di.C3.A9_administration">Bédié administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bédié administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 1995, Bédié overwhelmingly won re-election against a fragmented and disorganised opposition. He tightened his hold over political life, jailing several hundred opposition supporters. In contrast, the economic outlook improved, at least superficially, with decreasing inflation and an attempt to remove foreign debt.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coted%27Ivoire_Elections2002.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Coted%27Ivoire_Elections2002.png/220px-Coted%27Ivoire_Elections2002.png" width="220" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Coted%27Ivoire_Elections2002.png/330px-Coted%27Ivoire_Elections2002.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Coted%27Ivoire_Elections2002.png/440px-Coted%27Ivoire_Elections2002.png 2x" data-file-width="499" data-file-height="552" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coted%27Ivoire_Elections2002.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Election results of 2002 in Ivory Coast</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike Houphouët-Boigny, who was very careful in avoiding any ethnic conflict and left access to administrative positions open to immigrants from neighbouring countries, Bedié emphasized the concept of "Ivority" (<a href="/wiki/Ivoirit%C3%A9" title="Ivoirité">Ivoirité</a>) to exclude his rival <a href="/wiki/Alassane_Ouattara" title="Alassane Ouattara">Alassane Ouattara</a>, who had two northern Ivorian parents, from running for future presidential election. As people originating from foreign countries are a large part of the Ivoirian population, this policy excluded many people from Ivoirian nationality, and the relationship between various ethnic groups became strained which resulted in two civil wars in the following decades.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1999_coup">1999 coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1999 coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Similarly, Bedié excluded many potential opponents from the army. In late 1999, a group of dissatisfied officers staged a military <a href="/wiki/1999_Ivorian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1999 Ivorian coup d'état">coup</a>, putting General <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gu%C3%A9%C3%AF" title="Robert Guéï">Robert Guéï</a> in power. Bedié fled into exile in France. The new leadership reduced crime and corruption, and the generals pressed for austerity and openly campaigned in the streets for a less wasteful society.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gbagbo_administration">Gbagbo administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Gbagbo administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A presidential election was held in October 2000 in which <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Gbagbo" title="Laurent Gbagbo">Laurent Gbagbo</a> vied with Guéï, but it was peaceful. The lead-up to the election was marked by military and civil unrest. Following a public uprising that resulted in around 180 deaths, Guéï was swiftly replaced by Gbagbo. <a href="/wiki/Alassane_Ouattara" title="Alassane Ouattara">Alassane Ouattara</a> was disqualified by the country's Supreme Court, due to his alleged Burkinabé nationality. The existing and later reformed constitution [under Guéï] did not allow non-citizens to run for presidency. This sparked violent protests in which his supporters, mainly from the country's north, battled riot police in the capital, Yamoussoukro.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ivorian_Civil_War">Ivorian Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ivorian Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Ivorian_Civil_War" title="First Ivorian Civil War">First Ivorian Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Armed_insurgents,_First_Ivorian_Civil_War.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fe/Armed_insurgents%2C_First_Ivorian_Civil_War.jpg" width="300" height="173" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="173" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Armed_insurgents,_First_Ivorian_Civil_War.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A technical in the <a href="/wiki/First_Ivorian_Civil_War" title="First Ivorian Civil War">First Ivorian Civil War</a>, 2002–2007.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early hours of 19 September 2002, while the President was in Italy, there was an armed uprising. Troops who were to be demobilised mutinied, launching attacks in several cities. The battle for the main gendarmerie barracks in Abidjan lasted until mid-morning, but by lunchtime the government forces had secured the main city, Abidjan. They had lost control of the north of the country, and the rebel forces made their stronghold in the northern city of Bouake.</p>
<p>The rebels threatened to move on Abidjan again and France deployed troops from its base in the country to stop any rebel advance. The French said they were protecting their own citizens from danger, but their deployment also aided the government forces. It was not established as a fact that the French were helping either side but each side accused them of being on the opposite side. It is disputed as to whether the French actions improved or worsened the situation in the long term.</p>
<p>What exactly happened that night is disputed. The government claimed that former president Robert Guéï had led a coup attempt, and state TV showed pictures of his dead body in the street; counter-claims stated that he and fifteen others had been murdered at his home and his body had been moved to the streets to incriminate him. Alassane Ouattara took refuge in the German embassy; his home had burned down.</p>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/Gbagbo" title="Gbagbo" class="mw-redirect">Gbagbo</a> cut short his trip to Italy and on his return stated, in a television address, that some of the rebels were hiding in the shanty towns where foreign migrant workers lived. Gendarmes and vigilantes bulldozed and burned homes by the thousands, attacking the residents.</p>
<p>An early ceasefire with the rebels, which had the backing of much of the northern populace, proved short-lived, and fighting over the prime cocoa-growing areas resumed. France sent in troops to maintain the cease-fire boundaries,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> and militias, including warlords and fighters from Liberia and Sierra Leone, took advantage of the crisis to seize parts of the west.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2002_Unity_Government">2002 Unity Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 2002 Unity Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:059_French_Foreign_Legion.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/059_French_Foreign_Legion.JPG/300px-059_French_Foreign_Legion.JPG" width="300" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/059_French_Foreign_Legion.JPG/450px-059_French_Foreign_Legion.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/059_French_Foreign_Legion.JPG/600px-059_French_Foreign_Legion.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:059_French_Foreign_Legion.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Armed Ivorians next to a <a href="/wiki/French_Foreign_Legion" title="French Foreign Legion">French Foreign Legion</a> tank, 2004.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 2003, Gbagbo and rebel leaders signed accords creating a "government of national unity". Curfews were lifted and French troops patrolled the western border of the country. The unity government was unstable and the central problems remained with neither side achieving its goals. In March 2004, 120 people were killed in an opposition rally, and subsequent mob violence led to foreign nationals being evacuated. A later report concluded the killings were planned.</p>
<p>Though UN peacekeepers were deployed to maintain a <i>Zone of Confidence</i>, relations between Gbagbo and the opposition continued to deteriorate.</p>
<p>Early in November 2004, after the peace agreement had effectively collapsed following the rebels' refusal to disarm, Gbagbo ordered airstrikes against the rebels. During <a href="/wiki/2004_French%E2%80%93Ivorian_clashes" title="2004 French–Ivorian clashes">one of these airstrikes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bouak%C3%A9" title="Bouaké">Bouaké</a>, on 6 November 2004, French soldiers were hit and nine were killed; the Ivorian government has said it was a mistake, but the French have claimed it was deliberate. They responded by destroying most Ivoirian military aircraft (2 Su-25 planes and 5 helicopters), and violent retaliatory riots against the French broke out in Abidjan.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gbagbo's original mandate as president expired on 30 October 2005, but due to the lack of disarmament it was deemed impossible to hold an election, and therefore his term in office was extended for a maximum of one year, according to a plan worked out by the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>; this plan was endorsed by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> With the late October deadline approaching in 2006, it was regarded as very unlikely that the election would be held by that point, and the opposition and the rebels rejected the possibility of another term extension for Gbagbo.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> The UN Security Council endorsed another one-year extension of Gbagbo's term on 1 November 2006; however, the resolution provided for the strengthening of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Charles_Konan_Banny" title="Charles Konan Banny">Charles Konan Banny</a>'s powers. Gbagbo said the next day that elements of the resolution deemed to be constitutional violations would not be applied.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A peace accord between the government and the rebels, or <a href="/wiki/Forces_Nouvelles_de_C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire" title="Forces Nouvelles de Côte d'Ivoire">New Forces</a>, was signed on 4 March 2007, and subsequently <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Soro" title="Guillaume Soro">Guillaume Soro</a>, leader of the New Forces, became prime minister. These events have been seen by some observers as substantially strengthening Gbagbo's position.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_election">2010 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 2010 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ivorian_presidential_election,_2010" title="Ivorian presidential election, 2010">Ivorian presidential election, 2010</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:left; float:right; margin-right:9px; margin-left:2px;">
<tr>
<td align="right"><a href="/wiki/File:Alassane_Ouattara_a_Washington_en_Juillet_2011.png" class="image"><img alt="Alassane Ouattara a Washington en Juillet 2011.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alassane_Ouattara_a_Washington_en_Juillet_2011.png/200px-Alassane_Ouattara_a_Washington_en_Juillet_2011.png" width="200" height="237" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/Alassane_Ouattara_a_Washington_en_Juillet_2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/Alassane_Ouattara_a_Washington_en_Juillet_2011.png 2x" data-file-width="230" data-file-height="273" /></a></td>
<td align="right"><a href="/wiki/File:Daniel_Kablan_Duncan_2014.png" class="image"><img alt="Daniel Kablan Duncan 2014.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Daniel_Kablan_Duncan_2014.png/200px-Daniel_Kablan_Duncan_2014.png" width="200" height="214" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Daniel_Kablan_Duncan_2014.png/300px-Daniel_Kablan_Duncan_2014.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Daniel_Kablan_Duncan_2014.png/400px-Daniel_Kablan_Duncan_2014.png 2x" data-file-width="546" data-file-height="584" /></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Alassane_Ouattara" title="Alassane Ouattara">Alassane Ouattara</a><br />
<small><a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Ivory_Coast" title="President of the Ivory Coast" class="mw-redirect">President</a> since 2010</small></td>
<td align="center"><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Kablan_Duncan" title="Daniel Kablan Duncan">Daniel Kablan Duncan</a><br />
<small><a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_Ivory_Coast" title="Prime Minister of the Ivory Coast" class="mw-redirect">Prime Minister</a> since 2012</small></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The presidential elections that should have been organized in 2005 were postponed until November 2010. The preliminary results announced independently by the president of the Electoral Commission from the headquarter of Allasane due to controverses about fraud in that commission, consisting in majority and presided by Allasane supporters showed a loss for Gbagbo in favour of his rival, former prime minister <a href="/wiki/Alassane_Ouattara" title="Alassane Ouattara">Alassane Ouattara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thousands26_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thousands26-52"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The ruling FPI contested the results before the Constitutional Council, charging massive fraud in the northern departments controlled by the rebels of the <a href="/wiki/Forces_Nouvelles_de_C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire" title="Forces Nouvelles de Côte d'Ivoire">Forces Nouvelles de Côte d'Ivoire</a> (FNCI). These charges were contradicted by United Nations' observers (differently from Africa union Observers). The report of the results led to severe tension and violent incidents. The Constitutional Council, which consists of Gbagbo supporters, declared the results of seven northern departments unlawful and that Gbagbo had won the elections with 51% of the vote - instead of Ouattara winning with 54%, as reported by the Electoral Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-Thousands26_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thousands26-52"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the inauguration of Gbagbo, Ouattara, recognized as the winner by most countries and the United Nations, organized an alternative inauguration. These events raised fears of a resurgence of the civil war; thousands of refugees have fled the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Thousands26_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thousands26-52"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> sent <a href="/wiki/Thabo_Mbeki" title="Thabo Mbeki">Thabo Mbeki</a>, former President of South Africa, to mediate the conflict. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> adopted a common resolution recognising Alassane Ouattara as winner of the elections, based on the position of the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">Economic Community of West African States</a> (ECOWAS). ECOWAS suspended Ivory Coast from all its decision-making bodies<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> while the African Union also suspended the country's membership.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, a Colonel of the Ivory Coast armed forces, Nguessan Yao was arrested in New York in a year-long <a href="/wiki/U.S._Immigration_and_Customs_Enforcement" title="U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement">U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement</a> operation charging for procuring and illegal export weapons and munitions of 4,000 <a href="/wiki/9_mm" title="9 mm" class="mw-redirect">9 mm</a> handguns, 200,000 rounds of ammunition and 50,000 tear-gas grenades, in violation of UN embargo. Several other Ivory Coast officers were released for their diplomatic passports. His accomplice, Michael Barry Shor, an international trader, was located in Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_Civil_War">2011 Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 2011 Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Internally_Displaced_Persons_Duekoue_2011_Cote_dIvoire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Internally_Displaced_Persons_Duekoue_2011_Cote_dIvoire.jpg/220px-Internally_Displaced_Persons_Duekoue_2011_Cote_dIvoire.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Internally_Displaced_Persons_Duekoue_2011_Cote_dIvoire.jpg/330px-Internally_Displaced_Persons_Duekoue_2011_Cote_dIvoire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Internally_Displaced_Persons_Duekoue_2011_Cote_dIvoire.jpg/440px-Internally_Displaced_Persons_Duekoue_2011_Cote_dIvoire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2721" data-file-height="1716" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Internally_Displaced_Persons_Duekoue_2011_Cote_dIvoire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A shelter for internally displaced persons during the 2011 civil war.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Ivorian_Civil_War" title="Second Ivorian Civil War">Second Ivorian Civil War</a></div>
<p>The 2010 presidential election led to the <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%932011_Ivorian_crisis" title="2010–2011 Ivorian crisis" class="mw-redirect">2010–2011 Ivorian crisis</a> and to the Second Ivorian Civil War. International organizations reported numerous human rights violations by both sides. In the city of <a href="/wiki/Du%C3%A9kou%C3%A9" title="Duékoué">Duékoué</a>, hundreds of people were killed. In nearby <a href="/wiki/Blol%C3%A9quin" title="Bloléquin">Bloléquin</a>, dozens were killed.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> UN and French forces took military action against Gbagbo.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Gbagbo was taken into custody after a raid into his residence on 11 April. The country was severely damaged by the war, and observers consider that it will be a challenge for Ouattara to rebuild the economy and reunite Ivorians.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Administrative_divisions"></span><span id="Administrative_subdivisions"></span><span id="Regions_and_departments"></span></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Ivory_Coast" title="Geography of Ivory Coast">Geography of Ivory Coast</a></div>
<p>Ivory Coast is a country of western <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>. It borders <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guinea" title="Guinea">Guinea</a> in the west, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> in the north, <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> in the east, and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a> (Atlantic Ocean) in the south. The country lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/4th_parallel_north" title="4th parallel north">4°</a> and <a href="/wiki/11th_parallel_north" title="11th parallel north">11°N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/2nd_meridian_west" title="2nd meridian west">2°</a> and <a href="/wiki/9th_meridian_west" title="9th meridian west">9°W</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ivory_Coast&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p><br />
Ivory Coast is administratively reorganised into twelve districts with two district-level autonomous cites. The districts were further divided into regions, departments and sub-prefectures.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Under this new schema there were thirty-one regions.</p>
